<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_osmium" title="Template:Infobox osmium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_osmium" title="Template talk:Infobox osmium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_osmium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Osmium</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> ὀσμή <i>osme</i>, "smell") is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Os</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 76. It is a hard, brittle, bluish-white <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Platinum_group" title="Platinum group">platinum group</a> that is found as a <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth&#39;s crust">trace element</a> in alloys, mostly in <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> ores. Osmium is the densest naturally occurring element, with an experimentally measured (using x-ray crystallography) <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7004225900000000000♠</span>22.59&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup></span>. Manufacturers use its <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> with platinum, <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a>, and other platinum-group metals to make <a href="/wiki/Fountain_pen" title="Fountain pen">fountain pen</a> <a href="/wiki/Nib_(pen)#Nib_tipping" title="Nib (pen)">nib tipping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrical_contacts" title="Electrical contacts">electrical contacts</a>, and in other applications that require extreme durability and <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">hardness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaynes20114.25_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaynes20114.25-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth&#39;s crust">element's abundance</a> in the Earth's crust is among the rarest.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Osmium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Osmium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Osmium_1-crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Osmium_1-crop.jpg/170px-Osmium_1-crop.jpg" width="170" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Osmium_1-crop.jpg/255px-Osmium_1-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/Osmium_1-crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="289" data-file-height="262" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Osmium_1-crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Osmium, remelted pellet</div></div></div>
<p>Osmium has a blue-gray tint and is the densest <a href="/wiki/Stable_element" class="mw-redirect" title="Stable element">stable element</a>, approximately twice the density of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaynes20114.25_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaynes20114.25-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and slightly denser than <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Densities_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Densities-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Calculations of density from the <a href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" class="mw-redirect" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a> data may produce the most reliable data for these elements, giving a value of <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7004225620000000000♠</span>22.562<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.009&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gram_per_cubic_centimetre" title="Gram per cubic centimetre">g/cm<sup>3</sup></a></span> for iridium versus <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7004225870000000000♠</span>22.587<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.009&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup></span> for osmium.<sup id="cite_ref-Densest_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Densest-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Osmium is a hard but brittle <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> that remains <a href="/wiki/Lustrous" class="mw-redirect" title="Lustrous">lustrous</a> even at high temperatures. It has a very low <a href="/wiki/Compressibility" title="Compressibility">compressibility</a>. Correspondingly, its <a href="/wiki/Bulk_modulus" title="Bulk modulus">bulk modulus</a> is extremely high, reported between <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7002395000000000000♠</span>395</span> and <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7011462000000000000♠</span>462&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">GPa</a></span>, which rivals that of <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7011443000000000000♠</span>443&#160;GPa</span>). The hardness of osmium is moderately high at <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7009400000000000000♠</span>4&#160;GPa</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Because of its <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">hardness</a>, brittleness, low <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a> (the lowest of the platinum-group metals), and very high <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a> (the <a href="/wiki/List_of_elements_by_melting_point" class="mw-redirect" title="List of elements by melting point">fourth highest</a> of all elements, after only <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a>), solid osmium is difficult to machine, form or work.
</p>

<td><b>+4</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Osmium_dioxide" title="Osmium dioxide"><span class="chemf nowrap">OsO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Osmium(IV)_chloride" title="Osmium(IV) chloride"><span class="chemf nowrap">OsCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>+5</td>
<td><span class="chemf nowrap">OsF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>+6</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Osmium_hexafluoride" title="Osmium hexafluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">OsF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span></a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>+7</td>
<td><span class="chemf nowrap">OsOF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>+8</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Osmium_tetroxide" title="Osmium tetroxide"><span class="chemf nowrap">OsO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">Os(NCH<sub>3</sub>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p>Osmium forms compounds with <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> ranging from −2 to +8. The most common oxidation states are +2, +3, +4, and +8. The +8 oxidation state is notable for being the highest attained by any chemical element aside from iridium's +9<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and is encountered only in <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-selig_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-selig-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hassium" title="Hassium">hassium</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The oxidation states −1 and −2 represented by the two reactive compounds <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>[Os<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(CO)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">13</sub></span>]</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>[Os(CO)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>]</span> are used in the synthesis of osmium <a href="/wiki/Cluster_chemistry#Transition_metal_carbonyl_clusters" title="Cluster chemistry">cluster compounds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common compound exhibiting the +8 oxidation state is <a href="/wiki/Osmium_tetroxide" title="Osmium tetroxide">osmium tetroxide</a>. This toxic compound is formed when powdered osmium is exposed to air. It is a very volatile, water-soluble, pale yellow, crystalline solid with a strong smell. Osmium powder has the characteristic smell of osmium tetroxide.<sup id="cite_ref-mager_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mager-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Osmium tetroxide forms red osmates <span class="chemf nowrap">OsO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> upon reaction with a base. With <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, it forms the nitrido-osmates <span class="chemf nowrap">OsO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Holle_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holle-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Griffith_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffith-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Osmium tetroxide boils at 130&#160;°<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">C</a> and is a powerful <a href="/wiki/Oxidizer" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidizer">oxidizing</a> agent. By contrast, <a href="/wiki/Osmium_dioxide" title="Osmium dioxide">osmium dioxide</a> (OsO<sub>2</sub>) is black, non-volatile, and much less reactive and toxic.
</p><p>Only two osmium compounds have major applications: osmium tetroxide for <a href="/wiki/Staining" title="Staining">staining</a> tissue in <a href="/wiki/Electron_microscopy" class="mw-redirect" title="Electron microscopy">electron microscopy</a> and for the oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Alkenes" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkenes">alkenes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>, and the non-volatile osmates for <a href="/wiki/Sharpless_asymmetric_dihydroxylation" title="Sharpless asymmetric dihydroxylation">organic oxidation reactions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bozzola_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bozzola-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Osmium pentafluoride (OsF<sub>5</sub>) is known, but osmium trifluoride (OsF<sub>3</sub>) has not yet been synthesized. The lower oxidation states are stabilized by the larger halogens, so that the trichloride, tribromide, triiodide, and even diiodide are known. The oxidation state +1 is known only for osmium iodide (OsI), whereas several carbonyl complexes of osmium, such as <a href="/wiki/Triosmium_dodecacarbonyl" title="Triosmium dodecacarbonyl">triosmium dodecacarbonyl</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Os<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>(CO)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span></span>), represent oxidation state 0.<sup id="cite_ref-Holle_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holle-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Griffith_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffith-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In general, the lower oxidation states of osmium are stabilized by <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a> that are good σ-donors (such as <a href="/wiki/Amines" class="mw-redirect" title="Amines">amines</a>) and π-acceptors (<a href="/wiki/Heterocyclic_compound" title="Heterocyclic compound">heterocycles</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>). The higher oxidation states are stabilized by strong σ- and π-donors, such as <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite its broad range of compounds in numerous oxidation states, osmium in bulk form at ordinary temperatures and pressures resists attack by all acids and alkalis, including <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">aqua regia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Osmium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_osmium" title="Isotopes of osmium">Isotopes of osmium</a></div>
<p>Osmium has seven naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, six of which are stable: <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">184</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">187</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">188</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">189</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">190</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span>, and (most abundant) <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">192</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span>. <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">186</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span> undergoes <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a> with such a long <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7015200000000000000♠</span>(2.0<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>1.1)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>15</sup></span> years, approximately <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7005140000000000000♠</span>140<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span></span> times the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">age of the universe</a>, that for practical purposes it can be considered stable. Alpha decay is predicted for all seven naturally occurring isotopes, but it has been observed only for <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">186</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span>, presumably due to very long half-lives. It is predicted that <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">184</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">192</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span> can undergo <a href="/wiki/Double_beta_decay" title="Double beta decay">double beta decay</a> but this radioactivity has not been observed yet.<sup id="cite_ref-nubase_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">187</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span> is the descendant of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">187</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">Re</a></span> (half-life <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7018143902655999999♠</span>4.56<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>10</sup>&#160;years</span>) and is used extensively in dating terrestrial as well as <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteoric</a> <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocks</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Rhenium-osmium_dating" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhenium-osmium dating">rhenium-osmium dating</a>). It has also been used to measure the intensity of continental weathering over geologic time and to fix minimum ages for stabilization of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_mantle" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s mantle">mantle</a> roots of continental <a href="/wiki/Craton" title="Craton">cratons</a>. This decay is a reason why rhenium-rich minerals are abnormally rich in <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">187</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Os</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> However, the most notable application of Os isotopes in geology has been in conjunction with the abundance of iridium, to characterise the layer of <a href="/wiki/Shocked_quartz" title="Shocked quartz">shocked quartz</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_boundary" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary">Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary</a> that marks the extinction of the non-avian <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> 65 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Alvarez_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarez-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Osmium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Osmium was discovered in 1803 by <a href="/wiki/Smithson_Tennant" title="Smithson Tennant">Smithson Tennant</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Hyde_Wollaston" title="William Hyde Wollaston">William Hyde Wollaston</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The discovery of osmium is intertwined with that of platinum and the other metals of the <a href="/wiki/Platinum_group" title="Platinum group">platinum group</a>. Platinum reached Europe as <i>platina</i> ("small silver"), first encountered in the late 17th century in silver mines around the <a href="/wiki/Choc%C3%B3_Department" title="Chocó Department">Chocó Department</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The discovery that this metal was not an alloy, but a distinct new element, was published in 1748.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
Chemists who studied platinum dissolved it in <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">aqua regia</a> (a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acids</a>) to create soluble salts. They always observed a small amount of a dark, insoluble residue.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Proust" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Louis Proust">Joseph Louis Proust</a> thought that the residue was <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Victor_Collet-Descotils" class="mw-redirect" title="Victor Collet-Descotils">Victor Collet-Descotils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Fran%C3%A7ois,_comte_de_Fourcroy" title="Antoine François, comte de Fourcroy">Antoine François, comte de Fourcroy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Nicolas_Vauquelin" title="Louis Nicolas Vauquelin">Louis Nicolas Vauquelin</a> also observed iridium in the black platinum residue in 1803, but did not obtain enough material for further experiments.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Later the two French chemists Antoine-François Fourcroy and Nicolas-Louis Vauquelin identified a metal in a platinum residue they called ‘<i>ptène</i>’.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1803, <a href="/wiki/Smithson_Tennant" title="Smithson Tennant">Smithson Tennant</a> analyzed the insoluble residue and concluded that it must contain a new metal. Vauquelin treated the powder alternately with alkali and acids<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and obtained a volatile new oxide, which he believed was of this new metal—which he named <i>ptene</i>, from the Greek word <span lang="el" title="Greek language text">πτηνος</span> (ptènos) for winged.<sup id="cite_ref-griffith_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-griffith-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> However, Tennant, who had the advantage of a much larger amount of residue, continued his research and identified two previously undiscovered elements in the black residue, iridium and osmium.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> He obtained a yellow solution (probably of <i>cis</i>–[Os(OH)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2−</sup>) by reactions with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> at red heat. After acidification he was able to distill the formed OsO<sub>4</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-griffith_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-griffith-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> He named it osmium after <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>osme</i> meaning "a smell", because of the ashy and smoky smell of the volatile <a href="/wiki/Osmium_tetroxide" title="Osmium tetroxide">osmium tetroxide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-weeks_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weeks-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Discovery of the new elements was documented in a letter to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> on June 21, 1804.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">Uranium</a> and osmium were early successful <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a> reaction of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, giving enough yield to make the process economically successful. At the time, a group at <a href="/wiki/BASF" title="BASF">BASF</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Bosch" title="Carl Bosch">Carl Bosch</a> bought most of the world's supply of osmium to use as a catalyst. Shortly thereafter, in 1908, cheaper catalysts based on iron and iron oxides were introduced by the same group for the first pilot plants, removing the need for the expensive and rare osmium.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nowadays osmium is obtained primarily from the processing of <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> ores.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS-YB-2006_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-YB-2006-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Osmium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Platinum_nuggets.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Platinum_nuggets.jpg/220px-Platinum_nuggets.jpg" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Platinum_nuggets.jpg/330px-Platinum_nuggets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Platinum_nuggets.jpg/440px-Platinum_nuggets.jpg 2x" data-file-width="816" data-file-height="439" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Platinum_nuggets.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Native platinum containing traces of the other <a href="/wiki/Platinum_group" title="Platinum group">platinum group</a> metals</div></div></div>
<p>Osmium is one of the even-numbered elements, which puts it in the upper half of <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">elements commonly found in space</a>. It is, however, the <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth&#39;s crust">least abundant</a> stable element in Earth's <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a>, with an average mass fraction of 50&#160;<a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation" title="Parts-per notation">parts per trillion</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">continental crust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wede_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wede-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Osmium is found in nature as an uncombined element or in natural <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a>; especially the iridium–osmium alloys, <a href="/wiki/Osmiridium" title="Osmiridium">osmiridium</a> (osmium rich), and <a href="/wiki/Osmiridium" title="Osmiridium">iridosmium</a> (iridium rich).<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> deposits, the platinum group metals occur as <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfides</a> (i.e., (Pt,Pd)S)), <a href="/wiki/Telluride_(chemistry)" title="Telluride (chemistry)">tellurides</a> (e.g., PtBiTe), <a href="/wiki/Antimonide" title="Antimonide">antimonides</a> (e.g., PdSb), and <a href="/wiki/Arsenide" title="Arsenide">arsenides</a> (e.g., PtAs<sub>2</sub>); in all these compounds platinum is exchanged by a small amount of iridium and osmium. As with all of the platinum group metals, osmium can be found naturally in alloys with nickel or <a href="/wiki/Native_copper" title="Native copper">copper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within Earth's crust, osmium, like iridium, is found at highest concentrations in three types of geologic structure: igneous deposits (crustal intrusions from below), <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact craters</a>, and deposits reworked from one of the former structures. The largest known primary reserves are in the <a href="/wiki/Bushveld_Igneous_Complex" title="Bushveld Igneous Complex">Bushveld Igneous Complex</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-kirk-pt_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-pt-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> though the large copper–nickel deposits near <a href="/wiki/Norilsk#Norilsk-Talnakh_nickel_deposits" title="Norilsk">Norilsk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Basin" title="Sudbury Basin">Sudbury Basin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> are also significant sources of osmium. Smaller reserves can be found in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-kirk-pt_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-pt-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Alluvial" class="mw-redirect" title="Alluvial">alluvial</a> deposits used by <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Columbian">pre-Columbian</a> people in the <a href="/wiki/Choc%C3%B3_Department" title="Chocó Department">Chocó Department</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> are still a source for platinum group metals. The second large alluvial deposit was found in the <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Ural Mountains</a>, Russia, which is still mined.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS-YB-2006_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-YB-2006-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Osmium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Osmium_cluster.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Osmium_cluster.jpg/220px-Osmium_cluster.jpg" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Osmium_cluster.jpg/330px-Osmium_cluster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Osmium_cluster.jpg/440px-Osmium_cluster.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2060" data-file-height="1892" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Osmium_cluster.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Osmium <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystals</a>, grown by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_transport_reaction" title="Chemical transport reaction">chemical vapor transport</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Osmium is obtained commercially as a by-product from <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> mining and processing. During <a href="/wiki/Copper_extraction_techniques#Electrorefining" class="mw-redirect" title="Copper extraction techniques">electrorefining of copper</a> and nickel, noble metals such as silver, gold and the platinum group metals, together with non-metallic elements such as <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a> settle to the bottom of the cell as <i>anode mud</i>, which forms the starting material for their extraction.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs2008-summary_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs2008-summary-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MinYb2006_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MinYb2006-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Separating the metals requires that they first be brought into solution. Several methods can achieve this, depending on the separation process and the composition of the mixture. Two representative methods are fusion with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_peroxide" title="Sodium peroxide">sodium peroxide</a> followed by dissolution in <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">aqua regia</a>, and dissolution in a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> with <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kirk-pt_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-pt-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ullmann-pt_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-pt-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Osmium, ruthenium, rhodium and iridium can be separated from platinum, gold and base metals by their insolubility in aqua regia, leaving a solid residue. Rhodium can be separated from the residue by treatment with molten <a href="/wiki/Sodium_bisulfate" title="Sodium bisulfate">sodium bisulfate</a>. The insoluble residue, containing Ru, Os and Ir, is treated with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_oxide" title="Sodium oxide">sodium oxide</a>, in which Ir is insoluble, producing water-soluble Ru and Os salts. After oxidation to the volatile oxides, <span class="chemf nowrap">RuO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> is separated from <span class="chemf nowrap">OsO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> by precipitation of (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>RuCl<sub>6</sub> with ammonium chloride.
</p><p>After it is dissolved, osmium is separated from the other platinum group metals by distillation or extraction with organic solvents of the volatile osmium tetroxide.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The first method is similar to the procedure used by Tennant and Wollaston. Both methods are suitable for industrial scale production. In either case, the product is reduced using hydrogen, yielding the metal as a powder or <a href="/wiki/Metal_sponge" class="mw-redirect" title="Metal sponge">sponge</a> that can be treated using <a href="/wiki/Powder_metallurgy" title="Powder metallurgy">powder metallurgy</a> techniques.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Neither the producers nor the United States Geological Survey published any production amounts for osmium. In 1971, estimations of the United States production of osmium as a byproduct of copper refining was 2000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Troy_ounce" class="mw-redirect" title="Troy ounce">troy ounces</a> (62&#160;kg).<sup id="cite_ref-Appraisal_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Appraisal-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>  In 2017, the estimated US import of osmium for consumption was 90&#160;kg.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Osmium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Because of the volatility and extreme toxicity of its oxide, osmium is rarely used in its pure state, but is instead often alloyed with other metals for high-wear applications. Osmium alloys such as <a href="/wiki/Osmiridium" title="Osmiridium">osmiridium</a> are very hard and, along with other platinum-group metals, are used in the tips of <a href="/wiki/Fountain_pen" title="Fountain pen">fountain pens</a>, instrument pivots, and electrical contacts, as they can resist wear from frequent operation. They were also used for the tips of <a href="/wiki/Phonograph" title="Phonograph">phonograph</a> <a href="/wiki/Stylus" title="Stylus">styli</a> during the late 78 <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_per_minute" title="Revolutions per minute">rpm</a> and early "<a href="/wiki/LP_record" title="LP record">LP</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Single_(music)" title="Single (music)">45</a>" record era, circa 1945 to 1955. Osmium-alloy tips were significantly more durable than steel and chromium needle points, but wore out far more rapidly than competing, and costlier, <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">sapphire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> tips, so they were discontinued.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Osmium_tetroxide" title="Osmium tetroxide">Osmium tetroxide</a> has been used in <a href="/wiki/Fingerprint" title="Fingerprint">fingerprint</a> detection<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and in staining <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fatty</a> tissue for optical and <a href="/wiki/Electron_microscopy" class="mw-redirect" title="Electron microscopy">electron microscopy</a>. As a strong oxidant, it cross-links lipids mainly by reacting with unsaturated carbon–carbon bonds and thereby both fixes <a href="/wiki/Biological_membrane" title="Biological membrane">biological membranes</a> in place in tissue samples and simultaneously stains them. Because osmium atoms are extremely electron-dense, osmium staining greatly enhances image contrast in <a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">transmission electron microscopy</a> (TEM) studies of biological materials. Those carbon materials have otherwise very weak TEM contrast (see image).<sup id="cite_ref-Bozzola_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bozzola-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Another osmium compound, osmium ferricyanide (OsFeCN), exhibits similar fixing and staining action.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The tetroxide and its derivative  <a href="/wiki/Potassium_osmate" title="Potassium osmate">potassium osmate</a> are important oxidants in <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>. For the <a href="/wiki/Sharpless_asymmetric_dihydroxylation" title="Sharpless asymmetric dihydroxylation">Sharpless asymmetric dihydroxylation</a>, which uses osmate for the conversion of a <a href="/wiki/Double_bond" title="Double bond">double bond</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Vicinal_(chemistry)" title="Vicinal (chemistry)">vicinal</a> <a href="/wiki/Diol" title="Diol">diol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Barry_Sharpless" title="Karl Barry Sharpless">Karl Barry Sharpless</a> was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> OsO<sub>4</sub> is very expensive for this use, so KMnO<sub>4</sub> is often used instead, even though the yields are less for this cheaper chemical reagent.
</p><p>In 1898 an Austrian chemist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Auer_von_Welsbach" title="Carl Auer von Welsbach">Auer von Welsbach</a> developed the Oslamp with a <a href="/wiki/Electrical_filament" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical filament">filament</a> made of osmium, which he introduced commercially in 1902. After only a few years, osmium was replaced by the more stable metal <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>. Tungsten has the highest melting point among all metals, and its use in light bulbs increases the luminous efficacy and life of <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_lamp" class="mw-redirect" title="Incandescent lamp">incandescent lamps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-griffith_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-griffith-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The light bulb manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Osram" title="Osram">Osram</a> (founded in 1906, when three German companies, Auer-Gesellschaft, AEG and Siemens &amp; Halske, combined their lamp production facilities) derived its name from the elements of <b>os</b>mium and Wolf<b>ram</b> (the latter is German for tungsten).<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Like <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a>, powdered osmium effectively absorbs hydrogen atoms. This could make osmium a potential candidate for a metal-hydride battery electrode. However, osmium is expensive and would react with potassium hydroxide, the most common battery electrolyte.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Osmium has high <a href="/wiki/Reflectivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Reflectivity">reflectivity</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> range of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">electromagnetic spectrum</a>; for example, at 600 <a href="/wiki/%C3%85ngstr%C3%B6m" title="Ångström">Å</a> osmium has a reflectivity twice that of gold.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> This high reflectivity is desirable in space-based <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet-visible_spectroscopy" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultraviolet-visible spectroscopy">UV spectrometers</a>, which have reduced mirror sizes due to space limitations. Osmium-coated mirrors were flown in several space missions aboard the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>, but it soon became clear that the oxygen radicals in the <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> are abundant enough to significantly deteriorate the osmium layer.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The only known clinical use of osmium is <a href="/wiki/Synovectomy" title="Synovectomy">synovectomy</a> in arthritic patients in Scandinavia.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> It involves the local administration of osmium tetroxide (OsO<sub>4</sub>), which is a highly toxic compound. The lack of reports of long-term side effects suggest that osmium itself can be <a href="/wiki/Biocompatible" class="mw-redirect" title="Biocompatible">biocompatible</a>, though this depends on the osmium compound administered. In 2011, osmium(VI)<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and osmium(II)<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> compounds were reported to show anticancer activity <i>in vivo</i>, it indicated a promising future for using osmium compounds as <a href="/wiki/Anticancer" class="mw-redirect" title="Anticancer">anticancer</a> drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:84.5px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sharpless_Dihydroxylation_Scheme.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sharpless_Dihydroxylation_Scheme.png/200px-Sharpless_Dihydroxylation_Scheme.png" width="200" height="61" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sharpless_Dihydroxylation_Scheme.png/300px-Sharpless_Dihydroxylation_Scheme.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sharpless_Dihydroxylation_Scheme.png/400px-Sharpless_Dihydroxylation_Scheme.png 2x" data-file-width="1672" data-file-height="507" /></a></div></div>

Blackening reveals oxidation due to irradiation by oxygen atoms.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Osmium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Metallic osmium is harmless<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>  but finely divided metallic osmium is <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoricity" title="Pyrophoricity">pyrophoric</a><sup id="cite_ref-Appraisal_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Appraisal-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> and reacts with oxygen at room temperature, forming volatile osmium tetroxide. Some osmium compounds are also converted to the tetroxide if oxygen is present.<sup id="cite_ref-Appraisal_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Appraisal-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> This makes osmium tetroxide the main source of contact with the environment.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Osmium_tetroxide" title="Osmium tetroxide">Osmium tetroxide</a> is highly volatile and penetrates skin readily, and is very <a href="/wiki/Toxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Toxic">toxic</a> by inhalation, ingestion, and skin contact.<sup id="cite_ref-ToxOs_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ToxOs-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Airborne low concentrations of osmium tetroxide vapor can cause <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lung</a> congestion and <a href="/wiki/Human_skin" title="Human skin">skin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Human_eye" title="Human eye">eye</a> damage, and should therefore be used in a <a href="/wiki/Fume_hood" title="Fume hood">fume hood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mager_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mager-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Osmium tetroxide is rapidly reduced to relatively inert compounds by e.g. <a href="/wiki/Ascorbic_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascorbic acid">ascorbic acid</a><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyunsaturated">polyunsaturated</a> <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oils</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Corn_oil" title="Corn oil">corn oil</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Price">Price</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Osmium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Price">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Osmium is usually sold as a minimum 99.9% pure powder. Like other precious metals, it is measured by <a href="/wiki/Troy_weight" title="Troy weight">troy weight</a> and by <a href="/wiki/Grams" class="mw-redirect" title="Grams">grams</a>. Its price in 2018 is about $1,000 per troy oz, depending on the quantity, its purity and its supplier.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
